<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alternative_Energies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="2982" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/220px-Alternative_Energies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/330px-Alternative_Energies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/440px-Alternative_Energies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alternative_Energies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> are three emerging renewable sources of energy.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png"><img alt="a survey by isos shows that global support is strongest for solar and wind, followed by (in declining order) hydro, natural gas, coal and nuclear" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="1030" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/220px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/330px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/440px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global public support for different energy sources (2011) based on a poll by <a href="/wiki/Ipsos_MORI" title="Ipsos MORI">Ipsos Global @dvisor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Renewable energy</b> is energy that is collected from <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewable resources</a>, which are naturally replenished on a <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(time)" title="Orders of magnitude (time)">human timescale</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">waves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Renewable energy often provides energy in four important areas: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>/<a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">cooling</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">rural (off-grid)</a> energy services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren15_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren15-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on <a href="/wiki/REN21" title="REN21">REN21</a>'s 2017 report, renewables contributed 19.3% to humans' <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy consumption</a> and 24.5% to their generation of electricity in 2015 and 2016, respectively. This energy consumption is divided as 8.9% coming from <a href="/wiki/Biofuel#traditional" title="Biofuel">traditional biomass</a>, 4.2% as heat energy (modern biomass, geothermal and solar heat), 3.9% hydro electricity and 2.2% is electricity from wind, solar, geothermal, and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biomass</a>. Worldwide investments in renewable technologies amounted to more than US$286 billion in 2015, with countries such as <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_China" title="Renewable energy in China">China</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States" title="Renewable energy in the United States">United States</a> heavily investing in wind, hydro, solar and biofuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Globally, there are an estimated 7.7 million jobs associated with the renewable energy industries, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaics" title="Solar photovoltaics">solar photovoltaics</a> being the largest renewable employer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> As of 2015 worldwide, more than half of all new electricity capacity installed was renewable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianiea2015_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardianiea2015-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewable energy resources exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable_energy" title="Non-renewable energy">other energy sources</a>, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Rapid deployment of renewable energy and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> is resulting in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a>, and economic benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Energy_Agency_2012_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-International_Energy_Agency_2012-8">[8]</a></sup> The results of a recent review of the literature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> concluded that as <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> (GHG) emitters begin to be held liable for damages resulting from GHG emissions resulting in climate change, a high value for liability mitigation would provide powerful incentives for deployment of renewable energy technologies.  In international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_opinion_surveys" title="Public opinion surveys">public opinion surveys</a> there is strong support for promoting renewable sources such as solar power and wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-10">[10]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20 percent of energy supply. National renewable energy markets are projected to continue to grow strongly in the coming decade and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REN21_2013_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-REN21_2013-11">[11]</a></sup> Some places and at least two countries, Iceland and Norway generate all their electricity using renewable energy already, and many other countries have the set a goal to reach <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> in the future. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> the government decided to <a href="/wiki/Energy_transition" title="Energy transition">switch the total energy supply</a> (electricity, mobility and heating/cooling) to 100% renewable energy by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>While many renewable energy projects are large-scale, renewable technologies are also suited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> and remote areas and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_developing_countries" title="Renewable energy in developing countries">developing countries</a>, where energy is often crucial in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">human development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Former <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a> has said that renewable energy has the ability to lift the poorest nations to new levels of prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-14">[14]</a></sup> As most of renewables provide electricity, renewable energy deployment is often applied in conjunction with further <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a>, which has several benefits: Electricity can be converted to heat (where necessary generating higher temperatures than fossil fuels), can be converted into mechanical energy with high efficiency and is clean at the point of consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In addition to that electrification with renewable energy is much more efficient and therefore leads to a significant reduction in primary energy requirements, because most renewables don't have a steam cycle with high losses (fossil power plants usually have losses of 40 to 65%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewable energy systems are rapidly becoming more efficient and cheaper.  Their share of total energy consumption is increasing.  Growth in consumption of coal and oil could end by 2020 due to increased uptake of renewables and natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_solar_energy" title="Outline of solar energy">Outline of solar energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_renewable_energy_topics" title="Lists of renewable energy topics">Lists of renewable energy topics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">Sustainable energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="930" data-file-width="1870" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png/220px-Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png/330px-Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png/440px-Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Energy_Consumption_by_Source_2013.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">World energy consumption</a> by source. Renewables accounted for 19% in 2012.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3199" data-file-width="4830" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/220px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/330px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/440px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar" title="Tûranor PlanetSolar">PlanetSolar</a>, the world's largest solar-powered boat and the first ever solar electric vehicle to circumnavigate the globe (in  2012)</div></div></div>
<p>Renewable energy flows involve natural phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">plant growth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">geothermal heat</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> explains:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Renewable energy is derived from natural processes that are replenished constantly. In its various forms, it derives directly from the sun, or from heat generated deep within the earth. Included in the definition is electricity and heat generated from solar, wind, ocean, <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>, biomass, geothermal resources, and biofuels and hydrogen derived from renewable resources.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Renewable energy resources and significant opportunities for <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to other energy sources, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Rapid deployment of renewable energy and energy efficiency, and technological diversification of energy sources, would result in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a> and economic benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Energy_Agency_2012_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-International_Energy_Agency_2012-8">[8]</a></sup> It would also reduce environmental <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> caused by burning of fossil fuels and improve public health, reduce premature mortalities due to pollution and save associated health costs that amount to several hundred billion dollars annually only in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Renewable energy sources, that derive their energy from the sun, either directly or indirectly, such as hydro and wind, are expected to be capable of supplying humanity energy for almost another 1 billion years, at which point the predicted increase in heat from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a> is expected to make the surface of the earth too hot for liquid water to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroeder_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroeder-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> concerns, coupled with <a href="/wiki/2000s_energy_crisis" title="2000s energy crisis">high oil prices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, and increasing government support, are driving increasing renewable energy legislation, incentives and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">commercialization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-10">[10]</a></sup> New government spending, regulation and policies helped the industry weather the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">global financial crisis</a> better than many other sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obama_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-obama-24">[24]</a></sup> According to a 2011 projection by the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, solar power generators may produce most of the world's electricity within 50 years, reducing the emissions of greenhouse gases that harm the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_Sills_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben_Sills-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2011, small solar PV systems provide electricity to a few million households, and micro-hydro configured into mini-grids serves many more. Over 44 million households use <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> made in household-scale digesters for <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>, and more than 166 million households rely on a new generation of more-efficient biomass cookstoves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REN21_2011_14_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-REN21_2011_14-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a> has said that renewable energy has the ability to lift the poorest nations to new levels of prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-14">[14]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of energy supply. National renewable energy markets are projected to continue to grow strongly in the coming decade and beyond, and some 120 countries have various policy targets for longer-term shares of renewable energy, including a 20% target of all electricity generated for the European Union by 2020. Some countries have much higher long-term policy targets of up to 100% renewables. Outside Europe, a diverse group of 20 or more other countries target renewable energy shares in the 2020–2030 time frame that range from 10% to 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REN21_2013_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-REN21_2013-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewable energy often displaces conventional fuels in four areas: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">hot water</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a>, and rural (off-grid) energy services:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren15_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ren15-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Power generation</b></li></ul>
<dl><dd>By 2040, renewable energy is projected to equal coal and natural gas electricity generation. Several jurisdictions, including Denmark, Germany, the state of South Australia and some US states have achieved high integration of variable renewables. For example, in 2015 wind power met 42% of electricity demand in Denmark, 23.2% in Portugal and 15.5% in Uruguay. Interconnectors enable countries to balance electricity systems by allowing the import and export of renewable energy. Innovative hybrid systems have emerged between countries and regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Heating</b></li></ul>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">Solar water heating</a> makes an important contribution to <a href="/wiki/Renewable_heat" title="Renewable heat">renewable heat</a> in many countries, most notably in China, which now has 70% of the global total (180 GWth). Most of these systems are installed on multi-family apartment buildings and meet a portion of the hot water needs of an estimated 50–60 million households in China. Worldwide, total installed <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">solar water heating</a> systems meet a portion of the water heating needs of over 70 million households. The use of biomass for heating continues to grow as well. In Sweden, national use of biomass energy has surpassed that of oil. Direct geothermal for heating is also growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren53_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren53-28">[28]</a></sup>  The newest addition to Heating is from <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heat_pump" title="Geothermal heat pump">Geothermal Heat Pumps</a> which provide both heating and cooling, and also flatten the electric demand curve and are thus an increasing national priority<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-energy.gov_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-energy.gov-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Net_Zero_Foundation_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Net_Zero_Foundation-30">[30]</a></sup> (see also <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">Renewable thermal energy</a>).</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Transportation</b></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="500" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/220px-Soybeanbus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/330px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/440px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bus fueled by <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a></div></div></div>
<dl><dd><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">Bioethanol</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a>, mostly from <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> produced in <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">sweet sorghum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulosic biomass</a>, derived from non-food sources such as trees and grasses is also being developed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedstock" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a> for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_additive" title="Fuel additive">additive</a> to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Biofuel_in_the_United_States" title="Biofuel in the United States">USA</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> additive to reduce levels of particulates, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is the most common <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> in Europe.</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>A <a href="/wiki/Solar_vehicle" title="Solar vehicle">solar vehicle</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> powered completely or significantly by direct <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>. Usually, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> (PV) cells contained in <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> convert the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>'s energy directly into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_energy" title="Electric energy">electric energy</a>. The term "solar vehicle" usually implies that solar energy is used to power all or part of a vehicle's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vehicle_propulsion" title="Vehicle propulsion">propulsion</a>. <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">Solar power</a> may be also used to provide power for communications or controls or other auxiliary functions. Solar vehicles are not sold as practical day-to-day transportation devices at present, but are primarily demonstration vehicles and engineering exercises, often sponsored by government agencies. However, indirectly solar-charged vehicles are widespread and <a href="/wiki/Electric_boat" title="Electric boat">solar boats</a> are available commercially.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>Prior to the development of coal in the mid 19th century, nearly all energy used was renewable. Almost without a doubt the oldest known use of renewable energy, in the form of traditional biomass to fuel fires, dates from 790,000 years ago. Use of biomass for fire did not become commonplace until many hundreds of thousands of years later, sometime between 200,000 and 400,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fire_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fire-31">[31]</a></sup> Probably the second oldest usage of renewable energy is harnessing the wind in order to drive ships over water. This practice can be traced back some 7000 years, to ships in the Persian Gulf<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-32">[32]</a></sup> and on the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Moving into the time of recorded history, the primary sources of traditional renewable energy were human <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">labor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_power" title="Animal power">animal power</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_power" title="Water power">water power</a>, wind, in grain crushing <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-32">[32]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Firewood" title="Firewood">firewood</a>, a traditional biomass. A graph of energy use in the United States up until 1900 shows oil and natural gas with about the same importance in 1900 as wind and solar played in 2010.
</p><p>In the 1860s and '70s there were already fears that civilization would run out of fossil fuels and the need was felt for a better source. In 1873 Professor <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Mouchot" title="Augustin Mouchot">Augustin Mouchot</a> wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_6-1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_6-1-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The time will arrive when the industry of Europe will cease to find those natural resources, so necessary for it. Petroleum springs and coal mines are not inexhaustible but are rapidly diminishing in many places.  Will man, then, return to the power of water and wind? Or will he emigrate where the most powerful source of heat sends its rays to all?  History will show what will come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1885, <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">Werner von Siemens</a>, commenting on the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effect</a> in the solid state, wrote:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>In conclusion, I would say that however great the scientific importance of this discovery may be, its practical value will be no less obvious when we reflect that the supply of solar energy is both without limit and without cost, and that it will continue to pour down upon us for countless ages after all the coal deposits of the earth have been exhausted and forgotten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> mentioned the end of fossil fuel in the concluding paragraphs of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_Geist_des_Kapitalismus" title="Die protestantische Ethik und der Geist des Kapitalismus">Die protestantische Ethik und der Geist des Kapitalismus</a>, published in 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Development of solar engines continued until the outbreak of World War I. The importance of solar energy was recognized in a 1911 <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i> article: "in the far distant future, <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">natural fuels</a> having been exhausted [solar power] will remain as the only means of existence of the human race".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a> was published in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In the 1970s environmentalists promoted the development of renewable energy both as a replacement for the eventual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depletion_of_oil" title="Depletion of oil">depletion of oil</a>, as well as for an escape from dependence on oil, and the first electricity generating <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> appeared. Solar had long been used for heating and cooling, but solar panels were too costly to build solar farms until 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The IEA 2014 World Energy Outlook projects a growth of renewable energy supply from 1,700 gigawatts in 2014 to 4,550 gigawatts in 2040. Fossil fuels received about $550 billion in subsidies in 2013, compared to $120 billion for all renewable energies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_technologies">Mainstream technologies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power">Wind power</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1801" data-file-width="2693" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/220px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/330px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/440px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 845<span class="nowrap"> </span>MW <a href="/wiki/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm" title="Shepherds Flat Wind Farm">Shepherds Flat Wind Farm</a> near <a href="/wiki/Arlington,_Oregon" title="Arlington, Oregon">Arlington, Oregon</a>, US</div></div></div>
<p>Airflows can be used to run <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>. Modern utility-scale wind turbines range from around 600 kW to 5 MW of rated power, although turbines with rated output of 1.5–3 MW have become the most common for commercial use. The largest generator capacity of a single installed onshore wind turbine reached 7.5 MW in 2015. The power available from the wind is a function of the cube of the wind speed, so as wind speed increases, power output increases up to the maximum output for the particular turbine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA-42">[42]</a></sup> Areas where winds are stronger and more constant, such as offshore and high <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> sites, are preferred locations for wind farms. Typically <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">full load hours</a> of wind turbines vary between 16 and 57 percent annually, but might be higher in particularly favorable offshore sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind-generated electricity met nearly 4% of global electricity demand in 2015, with nearly 63 GW of new wind power capacity installed. Wind energy was the leading source of new capacity in Europe, the US and Canada, and the second largest in China. In Denmark, wind energy met more than 40% of its electricity demand while Ireland, Portugal and Spain each met nearly 20%.
</p><p>Globally, the long-term technical potential of wind energy is believed to be five times total current global energy production, or 40 times current electricity demand, assuming all practical barriers needed were overcome. This would require wind turbines to be installed over large areas, particularly in areas of higher wind resources, such as offshore. As offshore wind speeds average ~90% greater than that of land, so offshore resources can contribute substantially more energy than land stationed turbines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 2014 global wind generation was 706 terawatt-hours or 3% of the worlds total electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydropower">Hydropower</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">Hydropower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1768" data-file-width="2847" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/220px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/330px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/440px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> in China</div></div></div>
<p>In 2015 hydropower generated 16.6% of the worlds total electricity and 70% of all renewable electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Since water is about 800 times <a href="/wiki/Density_of_air" title="Density of air">denser than air</a>, even a slow flowing stream of water, or moderate sea <a href="/wiki/Swell_(ocean)" title="Swell (ocean)">swell</a>, can yield considerable amounts of energy. There are many forms of water energy:
</p>
<ul><li>Historically hydroelectric power came from constructing large hydroelectric dams and reservoirs, which are still popular in third world countries. The largest of which is the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a>(2003) in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a>(1984) built by Brazil and Paraguay.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Small_hydro" title="Small hydro">Small hydro</a> systems are hydroelectric power installations that typically produce up to <span class="nowrap">50 MW</span> of power. They are often used on small rivers or as a low impact development on larger rivers. China is the largest producer of hydroelectricity in the world and has more than 45,000 small hydro installations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Run-of-the-river_hydroelectricity" title="Run-of-the-river hydroelectricity">Run-of-the-river hydroelectricity</a> plants derive energy from rivers without the creation of a large <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoir</a>. The water is typically conveyed along the side of the river valley (using channels, pipes and/or tunnels) until it is high above the valley floor, whereupon it can allowed to fall through a penstock to drive a turbine. This style of generation may still produce a large amount of electricity, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Joseph_Dam" title="Chief Joseph Dam">Chief Joseph Dam</a> on the Columbia river in the United States.</li></ul>
<p>Hydropower is produced in 150 countries, with the Asia-Pacific region generating 32 percent of global hydropower in 2010. For countries having the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_electricity_production_from_renewable_sources" title="List of countries by electricity production from renewable sources">largest percentage</a> of electricity from renewables, the top 50 are primarily hydroelectric. China is the largest hydroelectricity producer, with 721 terawatt-hours of production in 2010, representing around 17 percent of domestic electricity use. There are now three hydroelectricity stations larger than 10 GW: the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in China, <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a> across the Brazil/Paraguay border, and <a href="/wiki/Guri_Dam" title="Guri Dam">Guri Dam</a> in Venezuela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi2012_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">Wave power</a>, which captures the energy of ocean surface waves, and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a>, converting the energy of tides, are two forms of hydropower with future potential; however, they are not yet widely employed commercially. A demonstration project operated by the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Renewable_Power_Company" title="Ocean Renewable Power Company">Ocean Renewable Power Company</a> on the coast of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, and connected to the grid, harnesses tidal power from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a>, location of world's highest tidal flow. <a href="/wiki/Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion" title="Ocean thermal energy conversion">Ocean thermal energy conversion</a>, which uses the temperature difference between cooler deep and warmer surface waters, has currently no economic feasibility.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_energy">Solar energy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/220px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/330px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/440px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of the 550-megawatt <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a> in California, US</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="583" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg/220px-Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg/330px-Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg/440px-Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Pricing_LCoE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Levelised Cost of utility-scale solar PV is in sharp decline around the world, at under 2c/kWh in some regions</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a>, radiant <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, is harnessed using a range of ever-evolving technologies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">solar heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> (CSP), <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">concentrator photovoltaics</a> (CPV), <a href="/wiki/Solar_architecture" title="Solar architecture">solar architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Solar technologies are broadly characterized as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_solar" title="Passive solar">passive solar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Active_solar" title="Active solar">active solar</a> depending on the way they capture, convert and distribute solar energy. Passive solar techniques include orienting a building to the Sun, selecting materials with favorable <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">thermal mass</a> or light dispersing properties, and designing spaces that <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">naturally circulate air</a>. Active solar technologies encompass <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal energy</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">solar collectors</a> for heating, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, converting <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> into <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> either directly using <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a> (PV), or indirectly using <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> (CSP).
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic system</a> converts light into electrical <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> (DC) by taking advantage of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Solar PV has turned into a multi-billion, <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">fast-growing</a> industry, continues to improve its cost-effectiveness, and has the most potential of any renewable technologies together with CSP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Concentrated solar power (CSP) systems use lenses or mirrors and tracking systems to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. Commercial concentrated solar power plants were first developed in the 1980s. <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy#Dish_designs" title="Solar thermal energy">CSP-Stirling</a> has by far the highest efficiency among all solar energy technologies.
</p><p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> said that "the development of affordable, inexhaustible and clean solar energy technologies will have huge longer-term benefits. It will increase countries' energy security through reliance on an indigenous, inexhaustible and mostly import-independent resource, enhance <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, reduce pollution, lower the costs of mitigating <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, and keep <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> prices lower than otherwise. These advantages are global. Hence the additional costs of the incentives for early deployment should be considered learning investments; they must be wisely spent and need to be widely shared".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-49">[49]</a></sup> Italy has the largest proportion of solar electricity in the world, in 2015 solar supplied 7.8% of electricity demand in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In 2016, after another year of rapid growth, solar generated 1.3% of global power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal_energy">Geothermal energy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal_electricity" title="Geothermal electricity">Geothermal electricity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">Renewable thermal energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/220px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/330px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/440px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steam rising from the <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station</a> in Iceland</div></div></div>
<p>High Temperature Geothermal energy is from <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> generated and stored in the Earth. Thermal energy is the energy that determines the <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of matter. Earth's geothermal energy originates from the original formation of the planet and from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of minerals (in currently uncertain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dye_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dye-56">[56]</a></sup> but possibly roughly equal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gando_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gando-57">[57]</a></sup> proportions). The <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">geothermal gradient</a>, which is the difference in temperature between the core of the planet and its surface, drives a continuous conduction of thermal energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the core to the surface. The adjective <i>geothermal</i> originates from the Greek roots <i>geo</i>, meaning earth, and <i>thermos</i>, meaning heat.
</p><p>The heat that is used for geothermal energy can be from deep within the Earth, all the way down to Earth's core – 4,000 miles (6,400 km) down. At the core, temperatures may reach over 9,000 °F (5,000 °C). Heat conducts from the core to surrounding rock. Extremely high temperature and pressure cause some rock to melt, which is commonly known as magma. Magma convects upward since it is lighter than the solid rock. This magma then heats rock and water in the crust, sometimes up to 700 °F (371 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>From <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a>, geothermal energy has been used for bathing since <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> times and for space heating since ancient Roman times, but it is now better known for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Low Temperature Geothermal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-energy.gov_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-energy.gov-29">[29]</a></sup> refers to the use of the outer crust of the earth as a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_Battery" title="Thermal Battery">Thermal Battery</a> to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">Renewable thermal energy</a> for heating and cooling buildings, and other refrigeration and industrial uses.  In this form of Geothermal, a <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heat_pump" title="Geothermal heat pump">Geothermal Heat Pump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ground-coupled_heat_exchanger" title="Ground-coupled heat exchanger">Ground-coupled heat exchanger</a> are used together to move heat energy into the earth (for cooling) and out of the earth (for heating) on a varying seasonal basis.  Low temperature Geothermal (generally referred to as "GHP") is an increasingly important renewable technology because it both reduces total annual energy loads associated with heating and cooling, and it also flattens the electric demand curve eliminating the extreme summer and winter peak electric supply requirements.  Thus Low Temperature Geothermal/GHP is becoming an increasing national priority with multiple tax credit support<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and focus as part of the ongoing movement toward Net Zero Energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Net_Zero_Foundation_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Net_Zero_Foundation-30">[30]</a></sup>  New York City has even just passed a law<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> to require GHP anytime is shown to be economical with 20 year financing including the Socialized Cost of Carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio_energy">Bio energy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bioenergy" title="Bioenergy">Bioenergy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2176" data-file-width="3264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/225px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/338px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/450px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel#Sugarcane_in_Brazil" title="Sustainable biofuel">Sugarcane plantation</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a> in Brazil</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="531" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/225px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/338px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/450px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">CHP power station</a> using wood to supply 30,000 households in France</div></div></div></div>
<p>Biomass is <a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biological material</a> derived from living, or recently living organisms. It most often refers to plants or plant-derived materials which are specifically called <a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">lignocellulosic biomass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biomass_Energy_Center_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biomass_Energy_Center-65">[65]</a></sup> As an energy source, biomass can either be used directly via combustion to produce heat, or indirectly after converting it to various forms of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>. Conversion of biomass to biofuel can be achieved by different methods which are broadly classified into: <i>thermal</i>, <i>chemical</i>, and <i>biochemical</i> methods. Wood remains the largest biomass energy source today;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online.wsj.com_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-online.wsj.com-66">[66]</a></sup> examples include forest residues – such as dead trees, branches and <a href="/wiki/Tree_stump" title="Tree stump">tree stumps</a> –, yard clippings, wood chips and even <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>. In the second sense, biomass includes plant or animal matter that can be converted into fibers or other industrial <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemicals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>. Industrial biomass can be grown from numerous types of plants, including <a href="/wiki/Miscanthus" title="Miscanthus">miscanthus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Switchgrass" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and a variety of tree species, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_palm" title="Oil palm">oil palm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>).
</p><p>Plant energy is produced by crops specifically grown for use as fuel that offer high biomass output per hectare with low input energy. Some examples of these plants are wheat, which typically yield 7.5–8 tonnes of grain per hectare, and straw, which typically yield 3.5–5 tonnes per hectare in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The grain can be used for liquid transportation fuels while the straw can be burned to produce heat or electricity. Plant biomass can also be degraded from <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> through a series of chemical treatments, and the resulting sugar can then be used as a first generation biofuel.
</p><p>Biomass can be converted to other usable forms of energy such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> gas or transportation fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>. Rotting garbage, and agricultural and human waste, all release methane gas – also called <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. Crops, such as corn and sugarcane, can be fermented to produce the transportation fuel, ethanol. Biodiesel, another transportation fuel, can be produced from left-over food products such as vegetable oils and animal fats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Also, biomass to liquids (BTLs) and cellulosic ethanol are still under research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> There is a great deal of research involving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algal_fuel" title="Algal fuel">algal fuel</a> or algae-derived biomass due to the fact that it's a non-food resource and can be produced at rates 5 to 10 times those of other types of land-based agriculture, such as corn and soy. Once harvested, it can be fermented to produce biofuels such as ethanol, <a href="/wiki/Butanol" title="Butanol">butanol</a>, and methane, as well as biodiesel and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. The biomass used for electricity generation varies by region. Forest by-products, such as wood residues, are common in the United States. Agricultural waste is common in <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> (sugar cane residue) and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (rice husks). Animal husbandry residues, such as poultry litter, are common in the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuels</a> include a wide range of fuels which are derived from <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>. The term covers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Solid_biofuels" title="Biofuels">solid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Ethanol" title="Biofuels">liquid</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Biogas" title="Biofuels">gaseous</a> fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Liquid biofuels include bioalcohols, such as bioethanol, and oils, such as biodiesel. Gaseous biofuels include <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_gas" title="Synthetic gas">synthetic gas</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">Bioethanol</a> is an alcohol made by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermenting</a> the sugar components of plant materials and it is made mostly from sugar and starch crops. These include maize, sugarcane and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">sweet sorghum</a>. The latter crop is particularly suitable for growing in dryland conditions, and is being investigated by <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> for its potential to provide fuel, along with food and animal feed, in arid parts of Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>With advanced technology being developed, cellulosic biomass, such as trees and grasses, are also used as feedstocks for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> additive to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_the_United_States" title="Ethanol fuel in the United States">United States</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a>. The energy costs for producing bio-ethanol are almost equal to, the energy yields from bio-ethanol. However, according to the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a>, biofuels do not address global warming concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eea-biofuels_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-eea-biofuels-75">[75]</a></sup> Biodiesel is made from <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_fat" title="Animal fat">animal fats</a> or recycled greases. It can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, or more commonly as a diesel additive to reduce levels of particulates, carbon monoxide, and hydrocarbons from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is the most common biofuel in Europe. Biofuels provided 2.7% of the world's transport fuel in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biomass, biogas and biofuels are burned to produce heat/power and in doing so harm the environment. Pollutants such as sulphurous oxides (SO<sub>x</sub>), nitrous oxides (NO<sub>x</sub>), and particulate matter (PM) are produced from the combustion of biomass; the World Health Organisation estimates that 7 million premature deaths are caused each year by air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-77">[77]</a></sup> Biomass combustion is a major contributor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_storage">Energy storage</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">Energy storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">Grid energy storage</a></div>
<p>Energy storage is a collection of methods used to <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">store electrical energy</a> on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grid_(electricity)" title="Grid (electricity)">electrical power grid</a>, or off it. Electrical energy is stored during times when production (especially from intermittent power plants such as renewable electricity sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>) exceeds consumption, and returned to the grid when production falls below consumption. <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a> is used for more than 90% of all grid power storage. Costs of lithium ion batteries are dropping rapidly, and are increasingly being deployed as fast acting sources of grid power (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">operating reserve</a>) and for domestic storage.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_and_industry_trends">Market and industry trends</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy commercialization</a></div>
<p>Renewable power has been more effective in creating jobs than coal or oil in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_renewables">Growth of renewables</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="638" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png/220px-GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png/330px-GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png/440px-GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GlobalREPowerCapacity-exHydro-Eng.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global growth of renewables through to 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REN21-GSR-2012_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-REN21-GSR-2012-81">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="482" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif/220px-Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif/330px-Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif/440px-Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparing worldwide energy use, the growth of renewable energy is shown by the green line<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From the end of 2004, worldwide renewable energy capacity grew at rates of 10–60% annually for many technologies. In 2015 global investment in renewables rose 5% to $285.9 billion, breaking the previous record of $278.5 billion in 2011. 2015 was also the first year that saw renewables, excluding large hydro, account for the majority of all new power capacity (134 GW, making up 53.6% of the total). Of the renewables total, wind accounted for 72 GW and solar photovoltaics 56 GW; both record-breaking numbers and sharply up from 2014 figures (49 GW and 45 GW respectively). In financial terms, solar made up 56% of total new investment and wind accounted for 38%.
</p><p>Projections vary. The EIA has predicted that almost two thirds of net additions to power capacity will come from renewables by 2020 due to the combined policy benefits of local pollution, decarbonisation and energy diversification. Some studies have set out roadmaps to power 100% of the world’s energy with wind, hydroelectric and solar by the year 2030.
</p><p>According to a 2011 projection by the International Energy Agency, solar power generators may produce most of the world's electricity within 50 years, reducing the emissions of greenhouse gases that harm the environment. Cedric Philibert, senior analyst in the renewable energy division at the IEA said: "Photovoltaic and solar-thermal plants may meet most of the world's demand for electricity by 2060 – and half of all energy needs – with wind, hydropower and biomass plants supplying much of the remaining generation". "Photovoltaic and concentrated solar power together can become the major source of electricity", Philibert said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_Sills_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ben_Sills-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014 global <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> capacity expanded 16% to 369,553 MW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEG-2014_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEG-2014-83">[83]</a></sup> Yearly wind energy production is also growing rapidly and has reached around 4% of worldwide electricity usage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea2014_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwea2014-84">[84]</a></sup> 11.4% in the EU,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-85">[85]</a></sup> and it is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_China" title="Wind power in China">Asia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a>. In 2015, worldwide installed photovoltaics capacity increased to 227 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatts" title="Gigawatts">gigawatts</a> (GW), sufficient to supply 1 percent of global <a href="/wiki/Electric_energy_consumption" title="Electric energy consumption">electricity demands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">Solar thermal energy</a> stations operate in the United States and Spain, and as of 2016, the largest of these is the 392 MW <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Electric_Generating_System" title="Ivanpah Solar Electric Generating System">Ivanpah Solar Electric Generating System</a> in California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The world's largest <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a> installation is <a href="/wiki/The_Geysers" title="The Geysers">The Geysers</a> in California, with a rated capacity of 750 MW. <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Brazil" title="Renewable energy in Brazil">Brazil</a> has one of the largest renewable energy programs in the world, involving production of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> from sugar cane, and ethanol now provides 18% of the country's automotive fuel. Ethanol fuel is also widely available in the United States.
</p>
