</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power#Experimental_solar_power" title="Solar power">Experimental solar power</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>Concentrated photovoltaics (CPV) systems employ sunlight concentrated onto photovoltaic surfaces for the purpose of electricity generation. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermogenerator" title="Thermogenerator">Thermoelectric</a>, or "thermovoltaic" devices convert a temperature difference between dissimilar materials into an electric current.</dd></dl>
<ul><li>Floating solar arrays</li></ul>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">Floating solar arrays</a> are PV systems that float on the surface of drinking water reservoirs, quarry lakes, irrigation canals or remediation and tailing ponds. A small number of such systems exist in France, India, Japan, South Korea, the United Kingdom, Singapore and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The systems are said to have advantages over photovoltaics on land. The cost of land is more expensive, and there are fewer rules and regulations for structures built on bodies of water not used for recreation. Unlike most land-based solar plants, floating arrays can be unobtrusive because they are hidden from public view. They achieve higher efficiencies than PV panels on land, because water cools the panels. The panels have a special coating to prevent rust or corrosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> In May 2008, the Far Niente Winery in Oakville, California, pioneered the world's first floatovoltaic system by installing 994 solar PV modules with a total capacity of 477 kW onto 130 pontoons and floating them on the winery's irrigation pond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Utility-scale floating PV farms are starting to be built. <a href="/wiki/Kyocera" title="Kyocera">Kyocera</a> will develop the world's largest, a 13.4 MW farm on the reservoir above Yamakura Dam in <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> using 50,000 solar panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Salt-water resistant floating farms are also being constructed for ocean use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The largest so far announced floatovoltaic project is a 350 MW power station in the Amazon region of Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Solar-assisted_heat_pump" title="Solar-assisted heat pump">Solar-assisted heat pump</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>A <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a> is a device that provides heat energy from a source of heat to a destination called a "heat sink". Heat pumps are designed to move <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> opposite to the direction of spontaneous heat flow by absorbing heat from a cold space and releasing it to a warmer one. A solar-assisted heat pump represents the integration of a <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">thermal solar panels</a> in a single integrated system. Typically these two technologies are used separately (or only placing them in parallel) to produce <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">hot water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref1_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref1-180">[180]</a></sup> In this system the solar thermal panel performs the function of the low temperature heat source and the heat produced is used to feed the heat pump's evaporator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref2_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref2-181">[181]</a></sup> The goal of this system is to get high <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_performance" title="Coefficient of performance">COP</a> and then produce energy in a more <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">efficient</a> and less expensive way.</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>It is possible to use any type of solar thermal panel (sheet and tubes, roll-bond, heat pipe, thermal plates) or <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_thermal_hybrid_solar_collector" title="Photovoltaic thermal hybrid solar collector">hybrid</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mono-crystalline_silicon" title="Mono-crystalline silicon">mono</a>/<a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon" title="Polycrystalline silicon">polycrystalline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_solar_cell" title="Thin-film solar cell">thin film</a>) in combination with the heat pump. The use of a hybrid panel is preferable because it allows covering a part of the electricity demand of the heat pump and reduce the power consumption and consequently the <a href="/wiki/Variable_cost" title="Variable cost">variable costs</a> of the system.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">Artificial photosynthesis</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>Artificial photosynthesis uses techniques including <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a> to store solar electromagnetic energy in chemical bonds by splitting water to produce hydrogen and then using carbon dioxide to make methanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Researchers in this field are striving to design molecular mimics of photosynthesis that utilize a wider region of the solar spectrum, employ catalytic systems made from abundant, inexpensive materials that are robust, readily repaired, non-toxic, stable in a variety of environmental conditions and perform more efficiently allowing a greater proportion of photon energy to end up in the storage compounds, i.e., carbohydrates (rather than building and sustaining living cells).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faunce_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-faunce-183">[183]</a></sup> However, prominent research faces hurdles, Sun Catalytix a MIT spin-off stopped scaling up their prototype fuel-cell in 2012, because it offers few savings over other ways to make hydrogen from sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algae_fuels" title="Algae fuels">Algae fuels</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>Producing liquid fuels from oil-rich varieties of algae is an ongoing research topic. Various microalgae grown in open or closed systems are being tried including some system that can be set up in brownfield and desert lands.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_aircraft" title="Solar aircraft">Solar aircraft</a></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="1474" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg/220px-Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg/330px-Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg/440px-Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Impulse_SI2_pilote_Bertrand_Piccard_Payerne_November_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/Solar_Impulse" title="Solar Impulse">Solar Impulse</a> 2</i> was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar-powered_aircraft" title="Solar-powered aircraft">solar-powered aircraft</a> to complete a <a href="/wiki/Circumnavigation" title="Circumnavigation">circumnavigation</a> of the world.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>An <a href="/wiki/Electric_aircraft" title="Electric aircraft">electric aircraft</a> is an aircraft that runs on <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motors</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>, with electricity coming from <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultracapacitors" title="Ultracapacitors">ultracapacitors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_beaming" title="Power beaming">power beaming</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Electric_battery" title="Electric battery">batteries</a>.</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Currently, flying manned electric aircraft are mostly experimental demonstrators, though many small <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicles</a> are powered by batteries. <a href="/wiki/Model_aircraft#Electric_power" title="Model aircraft">Electrically powered model aircraft</a> have been flown since the 1970s, with one report in 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noth_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noth-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> The first man-carrying electrically powered flights were made in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAWA74_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAWA74-188">[188]</a></sup> Between 2015–2016, a manned, solar-powered plane, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Impulse_2" title="Solar Impulse 2">Solar Impulse 2</a>, completed a circumnavigation of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batrawy_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Batrawy-189">[189]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Solar_updraft_tower" title="Solar updraft tower">Solar updraft tower</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>The Solar updraft tower is a renewable-energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_plant" title="Power plant">power plant</a> for generating electricity from low temperature solar heat. Sunshine heats the air beneath a very wide greenhouse-like roofed collector structure surrounding the central base of a very tall <a href="/wiki/Chimney" title="Chimney">chimney</a> tower. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> causes a hot air updraft in the tower by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimney_effect" title="Chimney effect">chimney effect</a>. This airflow drives <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> placed in the chimney updraft or around the chimney base to produce <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. Plans for scaled-up versions of demonstration models will allow significant power generation, and may allow development of other applications, such as water extraction or distillation, and agriculture or horticulture.  A more advanced version of a similarly themed technology is the <a href="/wiki/Vortex_engine" title="Vortex engine">Vortex engine</a> which aims to replace large physical chimneys with a <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortex</a> of air created by a shorter, less-expensive structure.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">Space-based solar power</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd>For either photovoltaic or thermal systems, one option is to loft them into space, particularly Geosynchronous orbit.  To be competitive with Earth-based solar power systems, the specific mass (kg/kW) times the cost to loft mass plus the cost of the parts needs to be $2400 or less.  I.e., for a parts cost plus rectenna of $1100/kW, the product of the $/kg and kg/kW must be $1300/kW or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>  Thus for 6.5 kg/kW, the transport cost cannot exceed $200/kg.  While that will require a 100 to one reduction, SpaceX is targeting a ten to one reduction, Reaction Engines may make a 100 to one reduction possible.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate">Debate</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_job" title="Green job">Green job</a></div>
<p>Renewable electricity production, from sources such as wind power and solar power, is sometimes criticized for being variable or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermittent_power_source" title="Intermittent power source">intermittent</a>, but is not true for concentrated solar, geothermal and biofuels, that have continuity. In any case, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> has stated that deployment of renewable technologies usually increases the diversity of electricity sources and, through local generation, contributes to the flexibility of the system and its resistance to central shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been "not in my back yard" (<a href="/wiki/NIMBY" title="NIMBY">NIMBY</a>) concerns relating to the visual and other impacts of some <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>, with local residents sometimes fighting or blocking construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> In the United States, the Massachusetts <a href="/wiki/Cape_Wind" title="Cape Wind">Cape Wind</a> project was delayed for years partly because of aesthetic concerns. However, residents in other areas have been more positive. According to a town councilor, the overwhelming majority of locals believe that the <a href="/wiki/Ardrossan_Wind_Farm" title="Ardrossan Wind Farm">Ardrossan Wind Farm</a> in Scotland has enhanced the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent UK Government document states that "projects are generally more likely to succeed if they have broad public support and the consent of local communities. This means giving communities both a say and a stake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> In countries such as Germany and Denmark many renewable projects are owned by communities, particularly through <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> structures, and contribute significantly to overall levels of renewable energy deployment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>The market for renewable energy technologies has continued to grow. <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> concerns and increasing in <a href="/wiki/Green_job" title="Green job">green jobs</a>, coupled with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_since_2003" title="Oil price increases since 2003">high oil prices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, oil wars, <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>, promotion of <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicles</a> and renewable electricity, nuclear disasters and increasing government support, are driving increasing renewable energy legislation, incentives and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">commercialization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-10">[10]</a></sup> New government spending, regulation and policies helped the industry weather the 2009 economic crisis better than many other sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obama_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-obama-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>While renewables have been very successful in their ever-growing contribution to electrical power there are no countries dominated by fossil fuels who have a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">plan to stop</a> and get that power from renwables. Only Scotland and Ontario have stopped burning coal, largely due to good natural gas supplies. In the area of transportation, fossil fuels are even more entrenched and solutions harder to find.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> It's unclear if there are failures with policy or renewable energy, but twenty years after the Kyoto Protocol  fossil fuels are still our primary energy source and consumption continues to grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Heat_Incentive_scandal" title="Renewable Heat Incentive scandal">Renewable Heat Incentive scandal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Car_Wash" title="Operation Car Wash">Operation Car Wash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biomass#Environmental_damage" title="Biomass">Biomass § Environmental damage</a></div>
<p>The ability of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a> to contribute to a reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions is limited because both biomass and biofuels emit large amounts of air pollution when burned and in some cases compete with food supply. Furthermore, biomass and biofuels consume large amounts of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Other renewable sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> have the advantage of being able to conserve water, lower pollution and reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.
</p>
